Combined effect of climate change and 
crude oil pollution on the blue mussel 
(Mytilus edulis) early larval development 
 
M.Sc. Thesis 
By Carla Rodrigo González 
 
August 2025 
 
Supervisors: 
Sinja Rist 
Researcher, DTU Aqua 
Section for Ocean and Arctic 
 
Torkel Gissel Nielsen 
Professor, DTU Aqua 
Section for Ocean and Arctic 
==PAGE:2==
1 
 
Abstract 
Coastal marine ecosystems are increasingly exposed to multiple stressors due to climate change 
and human activities. Mussels (e.g. Mytilus spp) are key species performing important ecosystem 
functions. This thesis evaluated the individual and combined effects of temperature (17, 19 and 
21.5°C for the Danish population and 2, 3.5 and 5.3°C for the Greenlandic population), salinity (24 
and 33 PSU) and oil exposure (0, 1, 10 μL L-1) on early developmental stages of Mytilus edulis from 
Denmark and Mytilus spp from Greenland. Fertilization, larval development, growth, clearance 
rate, survival and settlement were assessed. Results showed that high temperature and oil 
exposure negatively affected fertilization, development, and settlement, while low salinity 
sometimes mitigated these effects. The Greenlandic population showed greater resilience during 
fertilization and early survival than the Danish population, suggesting local adaptation. However, 
larval development failed under laboratory conditions for the Greenlandic population. The D-
shaped stage in the Danish population was particularly sensitive to the combination of high 
temperature and oil. Effects on veliger clearance rate, length, survival, and settlement on 
pediveliger larvae followed similar stressor patterns but showed more variable responses, 
possibly due to experimental limitations. Stress during early life stages can reduce recruitment 
and result in smaller adult populations. A decline in Mytilus abundance may affect key ecosystem 
functions such as nutrient recycling and disruption of marine food webs. These findings highlight 
the vulnerability of Mytilus larvae to multiple environmental stressors and the importance of 
understanding how multiple stressors affect larval stages to predict future impacts on populations 
and the ecosystem.  
Keywords 
Fertilization, larval growth, settlement, temperature, salinity, anthropogenic pollution, multiple 
stressors

1. Introduction 
Coastal marine ecosystems are facing multiple, co-occurring stressors driven by climate change 
and human activities. Among the most relevant factors are ocean warming, changes in salinity 
patterns, and the presence of anthropogenic pollutants (Intergovernmental Panel on Climate 
Change, 2023).  
Under climate change scenarios, sea surface temperatures are projected to increase by more than 
2 °C in the North Sea and North Atlantic by 2100 under high-emission pathways such as SSP5-8.5 
(Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change, 2023). Elevated temperatures have been shown to 
affect multiple levels of biological organization. At the individual level, in marine invertebrates, 
these effects include physiological and behavioural responses as well as molecular processes 
(Lazo & Pita, 2012; Mlouka et al., 2025; Apine et al., 2022; Lang et al., 2023). The energy and 
resource investment required to cope with thermal stress often involves trade-offs that can 
impair vital functions such as growth and reproduction (Clarke, 2003). At broader scales, warming 
can also impact population dynamics, community structure, and ecosystem functioning in marine 
environments (Doney et al., 2012). 
Global surface salinity patterns have intensified worldwide since the 1950s, with the Atlantic 
ocean becoming more saline due to increased evaporation, while fresher regions such as the 
Pacific and Southern Oceans have continued to freshen due to ice melt and increased 
precipitation (Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change, 2023). In the subpolar North Atlantic, 
a significant freshening trend has been observed since the mid-2010s (Holliday et al., 2020). 
However, regional salinity trends are likely influenced by ocean circulation variability 
superimposed on long-term changes (Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change, 2023). In the 
North Sea, a decline in salinity is projected, with the region becoming less saline under high-
emission scenarios (Ottersen et al., 2023; Ottersen et al., 2025). Salinity has demonstrated to 
affect marine organisms, involving osmoregulation, energy expenditure, and metabolic and 
oxidative stress (Bal et al., 2021; Freitas et al., 2017), potentially leading to effects on vital 
functions such as growth, feeding and reproduction.  
In addition to climate change related stressors, adverse impacts from human activities are 
expected to intensify (Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change, 2023). Oil is the largest 
primary energy source worldwide. In 2023, global oil supply was approximately 101.9 million 
barrels per day, with an estimated 75% was transported by sea (Gov, 2024). Maritime traffic is 
projected to increase by 240–1,209% by 2050. (Sardain et al., 2019). The main component of oil 
are polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons (PAHs), which are widespread environmental pollutants 
==PAGE:5==
4 
 
that bioaccumulate in the tissues of sessile, filter-feeding bivalves, causing a variety of sublethal 
effects (Grundy et al., 1996), including physiological alterations (Bakhmet et al., 2021). Although 
progress has been made in understanding the effect of oil, there are still uncertainties regarding 
their chronic impact at low concentrations and the sensitivity of vulnerable species and 
ecosystems (Beyer et al., 2020).  
Blue mussels (Mytilus edulis) are filter-feeding bivalve inhabiting temperate regions of both the 
northern and southern hemispheres. They are capable of withstanding wide fluctuations in 
salinity, desiccation, temperature and oxygen level (Gosling, 1992).  
Mussels play a key ecological role by filtering large volumes of water, enhancing organic matter 
sedimentation, and accelerating mineralization, thereby significantly contributing to nutrient 
recycling (Prins & Smaal, 1994) and supporting ecosystem structure by regulating the pelagic food 
web through bottom-up and top down controls (Nielsen & Maar, 2007), as they feed on plankton 
but also serve as an important food source for top trophic-level species (Brandner et al., 2017). 
Gamete release occurs in seawater, and, therefore, fertilization and embryonic development 
occur externally (Gosling, 1992; Widdows, 1991), making early stages directly exposed to 
environmental conditions.  
Larval stages are generally more sensitive to environmental stressors than adults (Mohammed & 
Mohammed, 2013). Numerous studies have documented strong negative effects of 
environmental factors such as salinity, temperature variations, and acidification (e.g. Brenko & 
Calabrese, 1969; Mlouka et al., 2020; Gazeau et al., 2010), as well as anthropogenic stressors like 
oil and metal contamination (e.g. Baussant et al., 2011; Strmgren et al., 1986; Strømgren & 
Nielsen, 1991), and their combined effects (e.g. Boukadida et al., 2021; Riba et al., 2016), on early 
stages of M. edulis. These stressors have been shown to impair biological responses such as 
fertilization success, survival, development and growth. This sensitivity can be attributed to their 
underdeveloped organs, permeable skin, smaller size, and immature detoxification systems. 
These factors, along with faster uptake and limited protective mechanisms, increase their 
vulnerability compared to adults (Mohammed & Mohammed, 2013). Mussel population strongly 
rely on recruitment and larval supply (Caley et al., 1996). As a result, biotic and abiotic stressors 
affecting on larval supply will influence the structure and dynamics of the mussel communities.   
Climate change is expected to alter ecosystems worldwide, for instance, leading to a reduction in 
zooplankton and phytoplankton biomasses of 11% and 6%, respectively, resulting in modifications 
of trophic level biomass through bottom-up control (Chust et al., 2014). This reduces the capacity 
of oceans to regulate climate through the biological carbon pump across 47% of the ocean 
==PAGE:6==
5 
 
surface, predominantly in tropical regions; while positive trophic amplification is projected in the 
Arctic and Antarctic oceans (Chust et al., 2014). However, research on combined effects is needed 
to anticipate abrupt ecosystem impacts, as interactions among factors are complex. A meta-
analysis reviewing 171 studies of combined stressors in marine and coastal systems found 
additive effects in 26%, synergistic interactions in 36%, and antagonistic interactions in 38% of 
cases (Crain et al., 2008). This complexity makes it challenging to predict climate change impacts 
based on single-stressor studies.   
In a previous systematic review I conducted on combined effects of climate change and 
anthropogenic pollutants on meroplanktonic species, I observed a growing trend toward studying 
such combined effects. However, a notable research gap exists regarding M. edulis, a species of 
ecological and commercial importance, particularly concerning the combined effects of climate 
change and polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons (Appendix A1). Therefore, the aim of this thesis is 
to investigate how temperature, salinity, and oil interact to affect the early developmental stages 
of the blue mussel. 
I  hypothesize that the combined exposure to high temperature, low salinity, and oil exposure will 
negatively affect early life stages of Mytilus. High temperature will negatively affect early life 
stages by increasing physiological stress and reducing development success. Low salinity is 
expected to disrupt osmoregulation leading to worse performance in early stages. Oil exposure 
is predicted to have toxic effects on larvae, also impairing performance. Moreover, I predict that 
the combined exposure of these stressors will result in synergistic and antagonistic interactions. 
Finally, I expect that responses would differ between Danish and Greenlandic populations, 
reflecting potential population and local adaptation. The Greenlandic population experiences 
more extreme and variable conditions, including fluctuating salinities and temperatures, which 
may have selected for greater adaptability and tolerance to combined stressors. In contrast, the 
Danish population from the Limfjord experiences more stable and moderate environmental 
conditions, potentially resulting in lower tolerance to such stress combinations.   
2. Methodology 
2.1. Organism collection and maintenance 
Approximately 150 adult mussels (Mytilus sp.) of 3.4 ± 1.5 cm (mean ± SD, n=150) were collected 
on April 7th, 2025, at Disko Island, Western Greenland (69°14'11.5"N, 53°32'34.0"W). These were 
cleaned of all encrusting epifauna. Mussels were transported by plane in plastic containers 
without seawater, but with cooling elements to maintain low temperatures, ensuring suitable 
conditions during transport to the DTU Aqua laboratory in Lyngby, Denmark. After transport, 
==PAGE:7==
6 
 
mussels were maintained in buckets of 10 L filled with seawater at 32.6 PSU, moderate aeration 
and 2°C (Appendix A2; Fig. 11a). They were fed 3 to 4 times per week with a mixed suspension of 
Rhodomonas salina (2 L at 500 cells mL-1) and Thalassiosira weissflogii (500 mL at 250 cells mL-1). 
The mussels were not genetically identified but based on the geographic distribution described 
by Mathiesen et al., (2017) they are most likely Mytilus edulis, Mytilus trossulus, or possibly a 
hybrid of the two.  
On April 24th, 2025, approximately 200 adults Mytilus edulis of 5.7 ± 0.7 cm (mean ± SD, n=60) 
were collected at the Danish Shellfish Centre in Nykøbing Mors, Denmark. After three days in a 
flow-through seawater tank, they were transported to the DTU Aqua laboratory in Lyngby in 
cooling boxes over a four-hour journey. Upon arrival, mussels were cleaned and maintained in 
buckets of 50 L at 32.6 PSU and 10°C, under moderate aeration. One third of these mussels were 
placed in a suspended net to distribute them throughout the water column (Appendix A2; Fig. 
11b). These mussels were fed R. salina (1.5 L at 500 cells mL-1), 3 to 4 times per week.  
In both cases, aeration was provided via air tubing connected to a constant-flow air pump. Twice 
a week, one quarter of the water volume was changed by siphoning out faeces and debris. 
Buckets were cleaned and water replaced about every two months. Water quality was regularly 
assessed to adjust the frequency or volume of changes as needed. 
Two microalgal cultures (R. salina and T. weissflogii) were maintained throughout the 
experimental period to ensure a continuous food supply (Appendix A2, Fig. 12). 
2.2. Spawning induction and culture maintenance 
Thermal shock was employed to induce spawning in 10 to 20 adult mussels, following the 
hatchery protocol described by Utting & Spencer (1991). This involved raising the water 
temperature from the control level by 10°C for approximately 20 minutes and gently stirring the 
water with a glass rod to provide mechanical stimulation. Subsequently, the mussels were placed 
in 10°C (Danish) and 2°C (Greenlandic) water for 10 minutes. The process was repeated until a 
sufficient number of males and females had spawned. To avoid synchronised spawning and 
undesired fertilisation, mussels were placed and exposed to temperature changes individually in 
100 mL glass beakers (Appendix A2, Fig. 13). 
Egg viability was assessed based on their morphological uniformity and roundness (Kamermans 
et al., 2013) while sperm was assessed based on their motility. Density was estimated using an 
inverted microscope (Olympus CK2). Gametes were used within 30 minutes to one hour, as 
recommended by Kamermans et al. (2013). Eggs and sperm were combined in 250 mL glass 
beakers at a ratio of approximately 1 egg to 100–200 sperm to minimise polyspermy, as suggested 
==PAGE:8==
7 
 
by Beaumont et al. (2004). Fertilisation success was monitored via a stereomicroscope (Leica 
M50): polar bodies typically became visible after about 40 minutes at 18°C (Danish) and 5 hours 
at 2°C (Greenlandic) (Fig. 1a), and the first cell divisions were observed between 90 and 100 
minutes at 18°C (Danish) and 7 hours at 2°C (Greenlandic) (Fig. 1b and c). Evaluation of 
fertilization success was based on the percentage of fertilised eggs and the uniformity of the early 
cell division. 
Figure 1. Early larval development stages of M. edulis (Danish population) observed: a) 40 minutes after 
fertilization polar bodies were observed; b) 70 minutes after fertilization first divisions were detected; c) 
110 minutes after fertilization, advanced stage of cell divisions/ early embryonic development; d) 72 h 
after fertilization, normal D-shape larvae; e) 6 weeks after fertilization, pediveliger larvae initiating the 
settlement process. 
 
When fertilization success was confirmed, selected cultures were diluted and transferred to three 
1.5 L glass beakers, maintained at constant temperature of 18 °C (Danish) and 2°C (Greenlandic), 
with a density between 5 – 10 larvae L-1 (Galley et al., 2010). Gentle airflow was added after 36 
hours. Larvae started being fed 48 hours post-fertilization with R. salina at a density of 10,000 
cells mL-1, following Riisgård et al. (2013). Larval development was monitored daily. Water 
exchange involved replacing one-third of the volume three times per week by filtering out water 
with a 20 μm filter, with a complete beaker cleaning conducted weekly to prevent microalgal 
==PAGE:9==
8 
 
sediment accumulation by slowly and carefully transferring the culture to a clean beaker. 
Settlement was observed after 6 weeks (Fig. 1d) 
2.3. Experimental design and set-up  
Figure 2. Diagram of experimental treatments applied across different larval stages of Mytilus spp in this 
thesis. Two populations (Danish and Greenlandic) were tested in separate experiments: fertilization and 
early development (both populations) and veliger and pediveliger experiments (Danish population). Each 
experimental combination included levels of temperature (°C), salinity (PSU) and oil exposure (μL L-1). 
Endpoints varied depending on larval stage. 
Different treatment combinations were established for each experimental phase, including 
variations in temperature, salinity, and oil concentration (Fig. 2; Table 1).  
Temperature and salinity ranges were based on the minimum and maximum values recorded in 
the natural habitats of each population during the expected spawning and larval development 
period (April to August). For the Danish populations, temperatures of 17, 19 and 21.5°C and 
salinities of 24 and 33 PSU were selected based on environmental data obtained from the 
VIB3708 Løgstør Bredning station (2009–2019) (DCE - Nationalt Center for Miljø Og Energi, n.d.) 
(Appendix A3.1). For the Greenlandic populations, temperature treatments of 2, 3.5 and 5.3°C 
and the same salinity levels (24 and 33 PSU) were chosen using data from the Greenland 
Ecosystem Monitoring database (2018–2024)(Greenland Ecosystem Monitoring - GEM, n.d.) 
(Appendix A3.2). The selected ranges also align with projected sea surface temperatures from the 
Coupled Model Intercomparison Project Phase 6 (CMIP6), under scenarios SSP1-2.6 and SSP5-8.5 
for the North Sea (Danish) and North Atlantic (Greenlandic) regions (Intergovernmental Panel on 
Climate Change, 2023).  
==PAGE:10==
9 
 
Based on the commonly accepted discharge limit of 15 ppm for oily water from ships, as regulated 
under international maritime standards, the concentrations selected for this experiment (1, and 
10 µL L-1) fall within a range considered environmentally relevant (International Maritime 
Organization, 2024).  
Experiments specifically were distinguished between Danish and Greenlandic mussel populations 
due to their distinct temperature tolerances.  
Table 1. Summary of experimental treatments applied across different larval stages of Mytilus spp in this 
thesis. Two populations (Danish and Greenlandic) were tested in separate experiments: fertilization and 
early larval development (both populations) and veliger and pediveliger experiments (Danish population). 
Each experimental combination included levels of temperature (°C), salinity (PSU) and oil exposure (μL L-1).
