The mean temperatures registered were 17.8 ± 0.05, 18.75 ± 0.3 and 21.49 ± 0.4 °C for the Danish 
treatments, and 1.71 ±0.2, 3.66 ± 0.3 and 5.03 ± 0.5°C for the Greenlandic treatments. The mean 
salinities registered as control were 32.8 ± 0.5 PSU (Filtered Sea Water from DTU aqua used for 
the experiments), whereas the low salinity treatment was consistently adjusted at 24 PSU.  
To prepare the treatments, portable insulated containers were used as water baths. These were 
filled with water and equipped with heaters connected to Inkbird temperature controllers, which 
regulated the desired temperature. In addition, temperature loggers were placed in each bath to 
continuously monitor temperature stability throughout the experiment. To ensure homogeneous 
==PAGE:11==
10 
 
temperature distribution within each bath, an air pump was used to maintain gentle water 
circulation (Appendix A2; Fig. 14). 
Due to the time required for thermal equilibration, the water baths were set up and pre-heated 
1–2 days in advance, allowing the system to reach stable and consistent target temperatures 
before the start of the experiments. 
Experimental vials or bottles were filled on the same day of the experiment with seawater 
adjusted to the target salinity and oil concentration. Salinity was measured and adjusted using a 
CTD sensor to ensure precision in each treatment. The oil treatment was prepared from a stock 
solution consisting of 75 µL of crude oil dissolved in 500 mL of seawater, which was stirred using 
a magnetic stirrer until fully homogenized, following the method of Almeda et al. (2021). The 
chemical composition of the exposure water in the oil treatments at 1 μL L-1 can be found in Rist 
et al. (2024). The appropriate volume of this solution was then added to each vial to reach the 
final desired oil concentration. 
2.4. Fertilisation experiment 
Prior to thermal shock induction to obtain gametes, glass vials were prepared adjusted to the 
corresponding salinity and oil concentration for each treatment (Table 1). Gametes were pipetted 
in the vials to achieve a density of 30 eggs mL-1 (15 mL, 450 eggs per vial), and these were and 
placed in their corresponding temperature water bath and left static and in the dark until the end 
of the experiment. Egg and sperm viability was verified previously under inverted microscope. 
Parallel control cultures were initiated and monitored to confirm fertilisation progress. Once 
fertilisation was evident (approximately 100 minutes for Danish mussels and 8 hours for 
Greenlandic mussels), the experiment was fixed using a final concentration of 0.5% buffered 
formalin. Fertilised and unfertilised eggs were counted immediately using a stereomicroscope 
(Leica M50). 
2.5. Danish population early development experiment 
Danish mussels 
After the fertilisation experiment, as eggs began to divide, 30 embryos mL-1 (15 mL, 450 embryos 
per vial) were pipetted into their respective treatment vials (adjusted to the corresponding 
salinity and oil concentration for each treatment) and placed in their corresponding temperature 
water bath. D-shaped larvae were expected to be reached between 48-72 hours post-fertilisation 
(Kamermans et al., 2013). During >72 hours of exposure the vials were kept static in the dark. 
Subsequently, they were fixed with 0.5% formalin and stored at 4°C. Photographs of 20 larvae per 
vial were taken for morphometric measurements under a stereomicroscope (Olympus SZX10) 
==PAGE:12==
11 
 
equipped with a camera (Olympus DP23). The number of normally developed larvae (D-shape) 
were counted relative to the total. Morphological abnormalities were classified based on His et 
al. (1997) (Fig. 2). This classification was simplified by grouping “incomplete shell” and 
“trochophore” categories due to high similarities, and the “convex hinge” category was excluded 
from the statistical analysis due to its infrequent observation in this thesis. 
Figure 2. M. edulis larvae abnormalities (classification based on His et al. 1997), found in the experiment 
after 72h under different treatments exposure post fertilization: a) D-shaped larvae; b) protruded mantle; 
c) convex hinge; d) asymmetric larvae; e) incomplete development.  
 
Greenlandic mussels 
In this experiment, complete development to D-shaped larvae was not achieved. Therefore, at 72 
hours, samples were fixed with 0.5% formalin, and the number of individuals (trochophores) was 
counted to calculate survival. 
2.6. Veliger experiments (Clearance rate, length and survival) 
The following experiments were only conducted with the cultures from the Danish population. At 
4 weeks of age, cultures were used for the experiment by filling 30 mL borosilicate bottles with 
10 mL of seawater, adjusted to the corresponding salinity and oil concentration for each 
treatment (Table 1), and containing larvae at a density of 3 individuals per mL. Bottles were placed 
in their corresponding temperature water bath. Larvae were fed R. salina at 10,000 cells mL-1 
every 48 hours. At 24 hours, a 3 mL subsample was extracted from each bottle and the 
concentration of R. salina (fixed with Lugol’s solution) was counted under an inverted microscope 
(Olympus CK2), using a Sedgewick-Rafter chamber, in order to determine the clearance rate (CR), 
following Riisgård et al. (2013) as:  
𝐶𝑅= 𝑉
𝑁𝑡ln (𝐶𝑜
𝐶𝑡
) 
Where 𝑉 is the volume of water, 𝑁 is the number of individuals, 𝑡 is the duration of the 
experiment and 𝐶𝑜 and 𝐶𝑡 are the initial and final cell concentrations, respectively. During 
exposure, except for subsample extraction and feeding, the bottles were kept static and in the 
dark. One week later, the complete sample was fixed, survival was determined (Leica M50), and 
==PAGE:13==
12 
 
larval length of at least 20 larvae per vial were measured  (Olympus SZX10 equipped with an 
Olympus DP23 camera). 
Algal control test 
To ensure that the experimental treatments (temperature, salinity, and oil exposure) did not 
directly affect R. salina survival or concentration independently of larval consumption, parallel 
control bottles were prepared. These control bottles were identical to the veliger feeding 
experiment bottles, containing 10 mL of seawater and R. salina at 10,000 cells mL-1, but without 
the addition of larvae. After 24 hours, R. salina concentration was measured under an inverted 
microscope (Olympus CK2) in these control bottles using the same methodology. 
2.7. Pediveliger settlement experiment 
Before the settlement experiment, a biofilm was created by placing a filter mesh in the adult 
mussel bucket one month in advanced. This biofilm was later used for the experiment as 
settlement substrate.  
At 9 weeks of age, the larval cultures were used for the experiment. Borosilicate bottles (20 mL) 
were filled with 15 mL of seawater, adjusted to the corresponding salinity and oil concentration 
for each treatment (Table 1), containing 10,000 cells mL-1 of R. salina. Larval density was 3 
individuals per mL (45 larvae per bottle). A piece of the settlement substrate (1.5 x 8 cm) was 
placed in each bottle. Bottles were incubated at their corresponding temperature in a water bath, 
kept static and in the dark. Every 48 hours, settlement was checked under a stereomicroscope 
(Leica M50) and larvae were fed with 10,000 cells mL-1 of R. salina. After 8 days, the number of 
settled larvae, and the number of non-settled larvae were determined (Leica M50). 
2.8. Statistical analysis 
All statistical analyses were conducted in R (version 4.2.0) (R Core Team, 2022). A Bayesian 
framework was primarily employed for models involving proportional variables, while frequentist 
approaches were utilized for continuous variables.  
For all the experiments, normality of the data was checked using the Shapiro-Wilk test for samples 
with fewer than 100 observations, and a Lilliefors test for larger samples. Homogeneity of 
variances was assessed with Levene’s test. Fertilization success (%) for both Danish and 
Greenlandic populations, survival (%) of both Greenlandic mussels (72h) and Danish veliger larvae 
(1 week) as well as normal development (%) and the classification of morphological abnormalities 
in the Danish population (protruded mantle, asymmetric larvae, incomplete development) were 
analysed using a Bayesian binomial regression model. Although continuous, the clearance rate 
was also analysed using a Bayesian model. The morphological abnormalities were represented by 
==PAGE:14==
13 
 
percentages in mutually exclusive categories, with each type analysed individually. The Bayesian 
approach was chosen due to the challenges in finding well-fitting models and because of its 
greater robustness and ability to account for variability with small sample sizes.  
The effects on the dependent variable were modelled as the individual, two-way and three-way 
interaction of the factors (temperature, salinity and oil exposure). Models were implemented 
with four Markov Chain Monte Carlo (MCMC) chains, 4000 iterations per chain (including 2000 
warmup iterations), except for the type of abnormality (8000 iterations per chain, 4000 warmup 
iterations). Model results were interpreted using estimates on the logit scale and their 95% 
credible intervals (95% CI). However, for easier interpretation, probabilities were calculated for 
reporting (Appendix C1.1).  
Larval length (µm) was modelled using a Generalized Additive Model for Location, Scale and 
Shape (GAMLSS) (Stasinopoulos & Rigby, 2007). A skew t type 1 distribution (ST1 family) was 
chosen. The mean (location parameter) of the larval length was modelled as a function of the 
interaction of the three factors, while the scale (variance) and shape parameters were modelled 
as constants (Appendix C1.2). The concentration of the algal control experiment was analysed 
using analysis of variance (ANOVA).  
Estimated marginal means (EMMs) were used to explore the conditional effects of the main 
factors and their two-way and three-way interactions from the model. Subsequent pairwise 
comparisons were then performed using these EMMs, statistically controlling for the influence of 
other factors (Appendix C1.3).  
All models were validated. For Bayesian models, standard diagnostics were checked (R-hat values, 
effective sample size, trace plots). For frequentist models, randomized quartile residuals were 
used to validate homogeneity of variance (Park et al., 2020). 
3. Results 
Detailed results and coefficients are shown in the Appendix B and C.  
3.1. Fertilization  
Danish population 
Under control conditions, the mean fertilisation success observed in the experiment was 92.3% 
(SD=8.42) (Appendix B1.1; B2.1), and the estimated probability of fertilisation was 92.6% (Fig. 3a; 
Appendix C2.1).  
Several treatments affected the fertilization success. When exposed to the high temperature 
(21.5°C) the probability of fertilization was reduced to 76% compared to the control temperature 
==PAGE:15==
14 
 
(17°C), holding other factors constant. Neither low salinity (24 PSU) nor oil exposure (1 µL L-1) 
alone had substantial overall effects on fertilization.  
The combined effect of high temperature (21.5°C) and low salinity (24 PSU) reduced the 
probability of fertilization to 77.3%, with the two stressors acting additively. Similarly, the 
combined effects of high temperature (21.5°C) and oil exposure (1 µL L-1) reduced the probability 
of fertilization to 72%, also reflecting additive effects. In contrast, the combined effect of low 
salinity (24 PSU) and exposure to oil (1 µL L-1) resulted in a more pronounced decrease, reducing 
the fertilization probability to 66.4%, indicating a synergistic interaction. 
The three-way interaction between temperature, salinity and oil (21.5°C, 24 PSU and 1 µL L-1 of 
oil) showed a substantial decrease to 72.5% probability of fertilization. This value is approximately 
50% higher than the expected additive effects, indicating an antagonistic interaction of these 
factors.  
Greenlandic population 
Under control conditions, the mean fertilization success observed was 87.9% (SD=1.11)(Appendix 
B1.2; B2.2) and the estimated probability was 88.4% (Fig. 3b; Appendix C2.2).  
The different treatments had distinct and statistically relevant effects on fertilization success in 
the Greenlandic population. Oil exposure (1 µL L-1) had a substantial positive effect, increasing 
the probability of fertilization to 97.7%. In contrast, high temperature (5.3°C) and low salinity (24 
PSU) alone did not show credible individual effects.  
However, the combined effect of high temperature (5.3°C) and oil exposure (1 µL L-1) resulted in 
a synergistic negative interaction, reducing fertilization probability to 87.6%. Although this may 
not seem a substantial decrease compared to the control, it represents a notable decrease 
compared to the expected additive effects. Similarly, low salinity (24 PSU) and oil exposure (1 µL 
L-1) demonstrated a synergistic interaction, reducing fertilization probability to 76.8%. No 
consistent three-way interaction was observed among temperature, salinity and oil.    
==PAGE:16==
15 
 
Figure 3. Fertilization (%) of Mytilus spp. under different salinities (33 and 24 PSU), oil exposures (0 and 
1 µL L-1 , left and right panels, respectively) and temperatures. (a) Results for Danish population (M. 
edulis) at 17 and 21.5°C after 100 minutes (b) Results for the Greenlandic population (Mytilus spp.) at 2 
and 5.3°C after 8 hours. Data represent mean values ± SE (n=3).  
3.2. Danish population early development experiments 
3.2.1. Normal development  
Several effects of the treatments were observed on normal development. The mean normal 
development percentage observed in the experiment was 66.4% (SD=3.61)(Appendix B1.3; B2.3), 
and the estimated probability was 66.3% (Fig. 4; Appendix C2.3).  
High temperatures (19 and 21.5°C) substantially reduced the probability of normal development, 
to 46.3% and 2.2%, respectively. Similarly, both levels of oil exposure (1 and 10 µL L-1) showed a 
substantial reduction of normal development to 47.2% and 49.3%, respectively (at salinity and 
temperature control conditions). Overall low salinity effect was positive but not statistically 
substantial.  
Interactions between factors significantly influenced normal development. The combined effect 
of low salinity (24 PSU) with both high temperature levels (19 and 21.5°C) showed a substantial 
antagonistic interaction. While specific pairwise comparison between 24 PSU and 19°C versus the 
control group was not substantially different, the combination of 24 PSU and 21.5°C significantly 
reduced normal development to 37.7%, but less than expected under additive effects.  
The combined effect of high temperature (19°C) and oil (1 µL L-1) also showed a significant 
antagonistic interaction, decreasing the probability of normal development to 47.3% although 
less than expected with additive effects. However, there was no interaction at higher levels (19°C 
and 10 µL L-1; 21.5°C and 1 and 10 µL L-1) meaning additive effects, substantially reducing the 
normal development probability (36.4%, 4% and 2.1%, respectively). This indicates that the 
interaction between factors is not static across all tested levels.  
==PAGE:17==
16 
 
Furthermore, the combination of low salinity (24 PSU) and oil at both concentration (1 and 10 µL 
L-1) showed a credible antagonistic interaction, resulting in normal development probabilities that 
were not statistically different from the control. 
A three-way interaction involving temperature, salinity, and oil exposure was also observed. 
Specifically, the combination of 19°C, 24 PSU, and 1 µL L-1 oil showed a credible synergistic 
interaction (68.6% of normal development), as the effect is greater than the expected additive 
effect, although not substantially different from the control group (17°C, 33 PSU and no oil). In 
contrast, combinations involving higher levels of temperature (21.5°C) and oil (10 µL L-1) did not 
show a substantial interaction, meaning negative, additive effects of the factors and substantially 
reducing the normal development probability (e.g. 39.5% normal development at 21.5°C, 10 µL L-
1 and 24 PSU), highlighting the complexity of the interaction of these factors. 
Figure 4. Normal development (%) of M. edulis after 72 hours of exposure post-fertilization to different 
salinities (33 and 24 PSU), temperatures (17, 19 and 21.5°C) and oil exposure (0, 1 and 10 µL L-1, panels 
from left to right). Data represent mean values ± SE (n=3). 
3.2.2. Type of abnormality  
Different treatments had significant effects on the type of abnormality. Among all the larvae 
classified as abnormal, 38.8% (SD=7.8) showed a protruded mantle, 40% (SD=13.2) were 
asymmetrical, and 21.1% (SD=11.7) were incomplete larvae (Appendix B1.3; B2.3). These 
proportions were estimated as 64.2%, 63.8% and 27.3%, respectively (Fig. 5; Appendix C2.3). The 
reason the estimated percentages do not sum to 100% is that the proportion of each abnormality 
type was analysed independently. This approach allowed to simplify and facilitate the analysis of 
the effects of the treatments, focusing on each abnormality type individually rather than on a 
comparison between them.  
High temperature (19 and 21.5°C) reduced the probabilities of developing a protruded mantle to 
27.2% and practically 0%, respectively, whereas increased the probability of incomplete 
==PAGE:18==
17 
 
development to 50.6% and 95.7%, respectively. Moreover, at 21.5°C the probability of asymmetry 
also decreased to 4.3%.  
Similarly, oil exposure (1 and 10 µL L-1) reduced the probability of protruded mantle development 
to approximately 2% in both cases. In contrast, it increased the probability of incomplete 
development to 47.3% and 58%, respectively. The probability of asymmetry is only significantly 
reduced to 20.4% at 10 µL L-1. 
In contrast, low salinity (24 PSU) increased the probability of developing a protruded mantle to 
62%, decreased to 23% the probability of asymmetry and showed no significant effect on the 
probability of incomplete development.  
The combination of low salinity (24 PSU) and high temperature (19 and 21.5°C) had no significant 
effect without oil exposure (for any of the three types). However, there was a significant 
interaction between these factors at 24 PSU and 21.5°C, where the observed abnormality 
proportion were different from the expected under additive effects, indicating a non-additive 
interaction. 
The combination of high temperature and oil exposure showed a substantial reduction of 
protruded mantle development (e.g. 18.4% and 14.7% at 19°C for 1 and 10 µL L-1, respectively) 
At 21.5°C and 1 and 10 µL L-1 the probability of both protruded mantle and asymmetry were 
substantially reduced to 3% at 1 µL L-1 and to 0% at 10 µL L-1. In contrast, the probability of 
incomplete development significantly increased to 100%. The combination of low salinity (24 
PSU) and oil (1 and 10 µL L-1) had no significant effect on the type of abnormality when compared 
to the control group.  
The combination of the three factors showed no substantial effect when compared to the control 
group. However, the impact of each factor was not lineal, indicating interactions between the 
factors.  
Overall, at control salinity (33 PSU), as the temperature increased the percentage of protruded 
larvae decreased, the asymmetrical and incomplete larvae increased at 19°C but at 21.5°C all 
larvae incompletely develop. This trend was more drastically observed under oil exposure: the 
more oil exposure, the less protruded mantle and more incomplete larvae whereas under low 
salinity less subtle effects were observed, keeping the proportions of incomplete development 
lower in comparison to control salinity.  
==PAGE:19==
18 
 
 
Figure 5. Proportion on abnormality type (protruded mantle, asymmetry, incomplete development) for M. 
edulis after 72 hours of exposure post-fertilization under different salinities (33 and 24 PSU), temperatures 
(17, 19 and 21.5°C) and oil exposure (0, 1 and 10 µL L-1, panels from left to right). Data represent mean 
values ± SE (n=3). 
3.2.3. Larval length  
Significant effects and interactions were detected across treatments on D-shaped larvae length 
after 72h of post-fertilization exposure, corresponding to their age. The mean control length 
observed was 92.7 µm (SD=11.5)(Appendix B1.3; B2.3), and the estimated control length was 
98.09 µm (Fig. 6; Appendix C2.3). 
High temperature showed significant negative effects on D-shaped larvae length at both 19°C and 
21.5°C, causing reductions of 7.4 µm and 33.2 µm, respectively. Oil exposure had no significant 
effect at 1 µL L-1 but significantly reduced larvae length by 7 µm at 10 µL L-1. Low salinity alone (24 
PSU) showed positive but not significant overall effects on larvae length.  
Interactions between factors significantly influenced larvae length. The combined effect of low 
salinity (24 PSU) and high temperature (at both 19°C and 21.5°C) showed significant antagonistic 
interaction, leading to no significant differences when comparing 24 PSU and 19°C to control 
conditions but a significant reduction of the length of 8.2 µm at 21.5°C and 24 PSU compared to 
control conditions (17°C, 33 PSU and no oil exposure), although this reduction is smaller than the 
expected with additive effects.  
In contrast, the combined effects of oil exposure and low salinity did not have any significant 
effect (nor interaction). However, high temperature and oil exposure demonstrated significant 
additive effects, reducing the larval size. As an example, a decrease of 36 µm of length was 
observed at 21.5°C and 10 µL L-1 compared to the control. 
==PAGE:20==
19 
 
A significant three-way interaction was observed only for the combined effect of 19°C, 24 PSU, 1 
µL L-1, which decreased larvae length by 10.53 µm compared to the expected additive effects of 
the three factors, suggesting a synergistic interaction. However, this reduction was not 
statistically significant when compared to the control group. All other three-way combinations 
showed additive effects, reducing significantly the larval length at 21.5°C, 24 PSU and 1 and 10 µL 
L-1 by 10.5 µm and 10.2 µm, respectively. 
Figure 6. Length (µm) of M. edulis after 72 hours of exposure post-fertilization under different salinities (33 
and 24 PSU), temperatures (17, 19 and 21.5°C) and oil exposure (0, 1 and 10 µL L-1, panels from left to right). 
Data represent mean values ± SE (n=3). 
3.3. Greenlandic early development: survival  
Significant and complex interactions were observed across the different treatments on survival 
of Greenlandic trochophores. The mean observed survival percentage after 72 hours in the 
control was 62.2% (SD=23.1)(Appendix B1.4; B2.4) and it was estimated at 62.3% (Fig. 7; Appendix 
C2.4). 
Positive and significant individual effects on survival were identified: low salinity increased the 
probability of survival to 70.8%. Similarly, oil exposure (1 µL L-1and 10 µL L-1) increased the 
probability of survival to 75% and 97.2%, respectively. High temperature exerted a negative but 
not significant effect (58.5% and 55.1% for 19°C and 21.5°C, respectively).  
Factor interactions also showed notable effects. The combined effect of temperature and oil, 
some combinations demonstrated significant positive effects on survival (e.g. 73% probability of 
survival at 1 µL L-1 and 21.5°C, and 74.5% at 10 µL L-1 and 21.5°C) compared to the control group. 
However, these interactions were characterized as antagonistic, indicating that the combined 
effect was less than the sum of the individual expected, especially due to the substantial positive 
effect of oil. Similarly, the combined effects of salinity and oil exposure showed significant positive 
effects on survival (e.g. 76.4% probability of survival at 1 µL L-1 and 24 PSU, and 75% at 10 µL L-1 
==PAGE:21==
20 
 
and 24 PSU), again suggesting an antagonistic interaction. The combination of high temperature 
(19°C) and low salinity (24 PSU) showed significant positive additive effects, increasing the 
probability of survival to 76.7%.  
The 3-way interaction showed significant positive additive effects at high temperatures (19°C and 
21.5°C) low salinity (24 PSU) and low oil exposure (1 µL L-1), increasing the probability of survival 
to 74% and 78%, respectively. At high oil exposure (19°C and 21.5°C, 24 PSU and 10 µL L-1) the 
factors interacted synergistically leading to an increase in the probability of survival to 66% and 
79.3%. 
 
3.4. Veliger experiments 
3.4.1. Clearance rate  
The observed mean control clearance rate was 0.165 mL ind-1 day-1 (SD=0.05)(Appendix B1.5; 
B2.5), whereas the estimated mean rate was 0.120 mL ind-1 day-1 (Appendix C2.5). None of the 
main factors (temperature, salinity and oil) showed overall significant effects on the clearance 
rate (Fig. 8). 
The combined effects of high temperature (21.5°C) and oil exposure (1 µL L-1) resulted in a 
significant increase of the clearance rate by 46.6%, interacting antagonistically. In contrast, 10 µL 
L-1 did not cause substantial effects on clearance rate. However, a synergistic interaction was 
found when combining low salinity (24 PSU) and oil exposure (10 µL L-1), leading to a statistically 
lower clearance rate than expected with additive effects (significantly reduced clearance rate by 
69.3% in comparison to the control). There were no substantial effects or interactions when 
combining high temperature and low salinity.  
 Figure 7. Survival (%) of Mytilus sp. trochophores after 72 hours of exposure post-fertilization under 
different salinities (33 and 24 PSU), temperatures (2, 3.5 and 5.3°C) and oil exposure (0, 1 and 10 µL  
L-1, panels from left to right). Data represent mean values ± SE (n=3). 
 
==PAGE:22==
21 
 
The three-way combination of high temperature, low salinity and oil exposure did not show 
significant effects nor interactions affecting the clearance rate.  
Figure 8. Clearance rate (mL ind-1 day-1) of 4 weeks old M. edulis after 24 h of exposure under different 
salinities (33 and 24 PSU), temperatures (17, 19 and 21.5°C) and oil exposure (0, 1 and 10 µL L-1, panels 
from left to right). Data represent mean values ± SE (n=3). 
Algal control test 
The algal control test demonstrated that none of the treatments significantly affected the 
concentration of R. salina (Fig. 9; Appendix B1.5; B2.5; C2.5).  
Figure 9. Concentration (cells mL-1) of R. salina after 24 hours of exposure under different salinities (33 and 
24 PSU), temperatures (17, 19 and 21.5°C) and oil exposure (0, 1 and 10 µL L-1, panels from left to right). 
This experiment served as an algal control test to assess the effects of the factors on the food source. Data 
represent mean values ± SE (n=3). 
3.4.2. Larval length   
The observed mean length of the veliger larvae was at 186.6 µm (Appendix B1.5; B2.5), and the 
estimated at 180.06 µm. Neither high temperature, low salinity nor oil exposure alone showed 
overall significant effects on larval size (Fig. 10; Appendix C2.5).  
==PAGE:23==
22 
 
The only combination that demonstrated significant interaction was high temperature and oil, 
specifically at 21°C and 10 µL L-1 of oil, reducing the length by 17.2 µm. This suggests a synergistic 
interaction, as the observed reduction in length was more pronounced than would be expected 
from the additive combination of their individual, non-significant effects. 
Additive effects were observed for the combinations of high temperature and low salinity, low 
salinity and oil exposure, and the three-way treatment, with no significant differences from the 
control.  
Figure 10. Veliger larval length (µm) of 4 weeks old M. edulis after 1 week of exposure under different 
salinities (33 and 24 PSU), temperatures (17, 19 and 21.5°C) and oil exposure (0, 1 and 10 µL L-1, panels 
from left to right). Data represent mean values ± SE (n=3). 
3.4.3. Survival  
The observed mean veliger survival was 81.13% (Appendix B1.5; B2.5) and the estimated 
probability of survival at 82.2%. Oil exposure at 10 µL L-1 showed significant negative effects, 
decreasing the probability of survival to 60.3%. Neither high temperature nor low salinity alone 
showed significant individual effects on veliger survival (Fig. 11; Appendix C2.5). 
Several two-way combinations strongly influenced veliger survival. The combination of low 
salinity (24 PSU) and high temperature (19°C) showed significant additive effects reducing the 
veliger survival to 64.8%. At 21.5°C, although no statistically substantial difference was found, 
these two factors interacted antagonistically (probability of survival to 83.1%). The combination 
of high temperature (19°C) and oil exposure (1 µL L-1) substantially decreased the probability of 
survival to 48.7%, interacting synergistically. In contrast, at 10 µL L-1 and both levels of 
temperature (19°C and 21.5°C) interacted antagonistically, resulting in higher survival rates than 
expected by additive effects, although not statistically different from the control (75.5% and 
84.0%, respectively). Similarly, the combination of low salinity (24 PSU) and oil (1 µL L-1) showed 
significant negative effects, reducing the veliger survival to 67%. Moreover, at 10 µL L-1 the factors 
==PAGE:24==
23 
 
interacted antagonistically, resulting in higher survival rates than expected by additive effects, 
although not statistically substantially different from the control (91.8%).  
 Pairwise combinations within the three-way interaction showed both significant positive and 
negative effects, without clear patterns. The interactions did not show consistent dependence on 
temperature or oil concentration.  The factors interacted antagonistically at 24 PSU, 1 µL L-1 and 
19°C (survival probability of 65.8%) but synergistically at 24 PSU, 10 µL L-1 and both 19°C and 
21.5°C (survival probability of 80.2% and 71.6%). 
 In summary, veliger survival was primarily negatively impacted by oil exposure alone. While 
neither high temperature nor low salinity individually affected survival, their two-way 
combinations exhibited varied influences, ranging from additive negative effects to synergistic or 
antagonistic interactions. The three-way interactions were complex and lacked a consistent 
pattern across factor levels, showing both synergistic and antagonistic effects depending on the 
specific combination of temperature, salinity, and oil concentrations. 
Figure 11. Veliger survival (%) of 4 weeks old M. edulis after 1 week of exposure under different salinities 
(33 and 24 PSU), temperatures (17, 19 and 21.5°C) and oil exposure (0, 1 and 10 µL L-1, panels from left to 
right). Data represent mean values ± SE (n=3). 
3.5. Pediveliger settlement experiment  
Relevant effects on larval settlement were detected across treatments. The observed mean 
settlement percentage was 18.5% (Appendix B1.6; B2.6), while it was estimated at 21.6% (Fig.12; 
Appendix C2.6). However, the 48% of the settlement observed occurred on detached filaments 
or debris aggregates, instead of the prepared experimental settlement substrate. 
Although oil exposure did not show a substantial overall effect, it exhibited credible negative 
impacts in specific conditions. For instance, at control salinity and without temperature stress, 
exposure to 10 µL L-1 oil substantially reduced the settlement probability to 8.5%. Similarly, high 
==PAGE:25==
24 
 
temperature (21.5 °C) did not have substantial overall effects but notably decreased settlement 
to 9.7% under control salinity and no oil exposure. Salinity did not show substantial effects.  
The combined effects of high temperature (21.5°C) and oil exposure (1 µL L-1) markedly reduced 
settlement to 9.3%. With higher oil concentration (10 µL L-1), a notable reduction in settlement 
(4.2%) was observed only at low salinity (24 PSU). These results suggest an antagonistic 
interaction between temperature and oil, as their combined effect was less severe than expected 
from their additive individual effects.  
In contrast, the triple combination of high temperature (21.5°C), low salinity (24 PSU) and oil 
exposure (1 µL L-1) resulted in a reduction of settlement to 7.7%, indicating additive negative 
effects.   
Figure 12. Settlement (%) of 9 weeks old M. edulis after 8 days of exposure under different salinities (33 
and 24 PSU), temperatures (17 and 21.5°C) and oil exposure (0, 1, 10 and 50 µL L-1, panels from left to right). 
Data represent mean values ± SE (n=3). 
4. Discussion  
Experimental limitations led to using a single female per population (and multiple males) 
therefore the observed variations represent an individual-level. Although this design limits 
population level predictions, it allows exploring inter-individual variability, which are supported 
by other studies that suggest that pooled gametes may mask selective success of stress-tolerant 
individuals (Eads et al., 2016). Additionally, all experiments were conducted with a limited 
number of replicates (n=3), which should be considered when interpreting the results. 
4.1. Fertilization 
The fertilization experiments for the Danish and Greenlandic populations showed similar and 
contrasting responses to the treatment. While low salinity alone had no substantial effects in 
either, its interaction with oil exposure showed a credible synergistic effect in both populations. 
However, contrasting responses were observed under other conditions. In the Danish population, 
==PAGE:26==
25 
 
high temperature significantly reduced fertilization probability, whereas in the Greenlandic 
population oil exposure unexpectedly increased it. These differences might reflect local 
adaptation or phenotypic plasticity between the two populations. 
The negative effect of temperature in the Danish population aligns with findings from Eads et al. 
(2016), who reported reduced fertilization at 24°C in Mytilus galloprovincialis, which is considered 
more heat-tolerant than M. edulis (Mlouka et al., 2020). Although the highest temperature tested 
was lower (21.5°C), it still significantly affected fertilization success. 
Salinity tolerance has been reported to be broader in M. edulis during fertilization than at later 
development stages (Bayne, 1965). This supports the limited effects of salinity on fertilization 
observed in both populations.  
Regarding oil exposure, the limited effect observed in the Danish population is supported by other 
studies. Baussant et al. (2011) found no effect on fertilization when only gametes of M. edulis 
were exposed to dispersed oil, but a reduction when both adults and gametes were exposed. 
Similarly, Lewis & Galloway (2009) showed that adult exposure caused DNA damage in sperm in 
M. edulis and the polychate Arenicola marina, which did not affect fertilization success but led to 
developmental abnormalities. They also observed a capacity for DNA integrity repair in sperm 
cells, although this was lower compared to somatic cells. 
Interestingly, in the Greenlandic population, oil exposure enhanced fertilization, potentially 
reflecting a hormetic response, a phenomenon in which low doses of a stressor induce beneficial 
or adaptive responses, and has been reported in a wide range of organisms (Rix et al., 2022). A 
similar pattern was described by Strømgren et al. (1986), who observed enhanced shell growth in 
M. edulis at 0.25 to 0.5 mg L-1 concentrations of microencapsulated hydrocarbons, which was 
later reduced when exposed to higher concentrations (4 mg L-1). Although the concentration used 
in this experiment was 1 µL L⁻¹, it cannot be directly compared due to differences in oil type and 
preparation. While Strømgren et al. (1986) used microencapsulated oil, in this experiment 
dispersed crude oil was used, which may differ in composition, solubility and bioavailability.  
As oil mainly consist of PAHs, its behaviour could be similar to other hydrophobic pollutants like 
UV filters (for example, 4-MBC and BP-3). It has been reported that their toxicity tends to increase 
at suboptimal salinity levels in M. galloprovincialis (Cuccaro et al., 2022). Even though the salinity 
range used in this experiment falls within the species tolerance, the polar body formation stage 
is particularly sensitive to low salinities (Bayne, 1965). This could explain an increased sensitivity 
in both populations when low salinity interacted synergistically with oil, and the reduced 
==PAGE:27==
26 
 
fertilization observed under the combined effects of low salinity and high temperature in the 
Danish population.  
In the Greenlandic population, a synergistic effect between high temperature and oil was also 
observed. This aligns with findings of Dose et al. (2024), who reported reduced fertilization in M. 
galloprovincialis when exposed to high temperature (24°C) and nanoparticles of titanium dioxide, 
which interacted synergistically. Although pollutants are different, this makes evident how 
combined stressors can strongly affect early developmental stages.  
These results highlight the complexity of stressor interactions on early life stages, which will be 
further explored in the next developmental experiments.  
4.2. Danish population early development experiment 
Normal development, abnormality type and larval length 
High temperature substantially induced abnormal development and strongly affected both the 
types of abnormalities and larval length in M. edulis larvae.  
Regarding the observed abnormalities, at control salinity (33 PSU), the proportion of larvae with 
protruded mantle, considered a relatively mild deformation, decreased as temperature and oil 
exposure increased. Instead, more severe abnormalities became dominant, such as asymmetric 
larvae, which exhibit marked body distortions that may impair swimming, and incomplete larvae, 
typically arrested at an early trochophore stage. At 21.5°C, nearly all larvae displayed incomplete 
development regardless of oil concentration, indicating a strong arrest in development under 
thermal stress. Consistent with these results, high temperature reduced the larval length, as 
abnormal larvae, especially asymmetrical and incompletely developed tend to be smaller. 
These findings align with previous studies on M. edulis by Boukadida et al. (2021), who observed 
98.5% of abnormal development at 22°C, with most of larvae showing developmental arrest. 
Mlouka et al. (2025) reported inhibition of the enzyme acetylcholinesterase under 22°C in Mytilus 
in early larval stages, which might impair neuromuscular function, and therefore affect survival 
and development. Similarly,  Mlouka et al. (2020) linked abnormal development at 22°C to altered 
expression of genes involved in the heat shock response, translation, cytoskeleton organization 
and immune function. These molecular alterations may explain the developmental abnormalities 
observed under high temperature, especially the changes in expression related to expression of 
cytoskeleton-related genes, since these are involved in the process of shell mineralization 
controlled by the mantle epithelium (Mlouka et al., 2020; Weiss et al., 2006).  These findings also 
support the negative effect of high temperature on larval length, since, as mentioned earlier, 
developmental arrest or abnormalities can result in smaller larvae. 
==PAGE:28==
27 
 
Normal development, abnormality type and larval length were also affected negatively by oil 
exposure. This supports the observations of Baussant et al. (2011), who showed that direct 
exposure of M. edulis embryos to dispersed North Sea oil (0.25 mg L-1) led to a significant reduced 
growth after 2 and 7 days. Although no differences in larval size were found after 21 days, the 
early growth inhibition suggests a strong initial sensitivity to oil exposure. The increase in 
abnormal development observed by Lewis and Galloway (2000) linked to the percentage of DNA 
damage caused by PAH (benzo(a)pyrene) and another cancerogenic agent (Methyl methane 
sulfonate) on M. edulis and A. marina aligns with the results and could also explain the smaller 
sizes observed. 
In contrast, salinity showed a mitigating effect, buffering the effects of high temperature and oil 
exposure reducing the abnormal development and showing less pronounced variations in the 
proportions of abnormal types across treatments. Moreover, low salinity showed a slight non-
significant positive effect on larval length. These findings differ from previous studies in other 
Mytilus species. Nadella et al. (2013) reported higher percentage of normally developed larvae of 
M. trossulus and M. galloprovincialis at 34 PSU compared to 24 PSU. Similarly, Hrs-Brenko et al. 
(1977) found minimal lead toxicity under optimal salinity conditions in M. galloprovincialis (34.8 
‰). However, in this experiment, larvae exposed to 24 PSU showed higher developmental 
success than those at 33 PSU across most treatments. This may suggest that 24 PSU is closer to 
the optimal salinity for M. edulis at this early developmental stage, likely influenced by the specific 
environmental origin of the population.  
The Danish population used in this experiment originates from the Limfjord, a brackish water 
system with salinities ranging between 20 and 30 PSU. Mussels in this region are farmed and 
naturally exposed to salinity conditions in a semi closed fjord which may lead to local adaptation 
or acclimatation to brackish conditions. This might have shaped the early developmental 
tolerance of this population, making 24 PSU closer to their optimal range than 33 PSU. Salinity 
tolerance is known to vary across populations. Bayne (1965) demonstrated that M. edulis larvae 
from areas of full salinity had narrow salinity optimum for growth at 30-33‰, whereas larvae 
from areas of reduced salinity exhibited a lower salinity optimum and greater tolerance ranges. 
These differences also interact with temperature. This context may explain the antagonistic 
interaction of low salinity when combined with high temperature and oil exposure, mitigating 
their negative effects observed under control salinity conditions (33 PSU). This highlights that 
both developmental stage and population-specific adaptations influence the salinity tolerance 
and the interactions with other stressors. 
==PAGE:29==
28 
 
Moreover, the relative weight of temperature and salinity in explaining variation appears more 
balanced in this experiment than previously described. His et al. (1989) reported that in M. 
galloprovincialis, temperature accounted for 85–89.5% of developmental variability, while 
salinity explained only 10–14%. While this pattern is supported (Hrs-Brenko et al., 1977), it is 
contrasted by Widdows (1991). The present results suggest that under these specific 
experimental conditions, salinity may play a significant role in modulating the effects of 
temperature and oil exposure. Similarly, Boukadida et al., (2024) found that salinity can modulate 
the formation of abnormalities when combined with other pollutants.   
An antagonistic interaction between temperature and oil was also observed for normal 
development, but only at lower levels (19 °C and 1 µL L⁻¹), whereas at higher levels (21.5 °C and 
10 µL L⁻¹) the effects were additive. This combination decreased the probability of protruded 
mantle and asymmetry and significantly increased developmental arrest. Larval length also 
decreased under these conditions. The combined negative effects of high temperature and other 
pollutants on Mytilus larval development of have been reported before. Boukadida et al. (2016, 
2021) reported additive negative effects when combining high temperature (20 and 24°C) with 
different metals (Cu and Ag), increasing abnormal development. Mlouka et al. (2025) also 
reported in larvae exposed to high temperature (22°C) and copper, an increase of circular 
trajectories (instead of the typical rectilinear movement of normal larvae), a decrease in the 
swimming speed, and significant inhibition of acetylcholinesterase. These effects may be linked 
to abnormal morphology and reduced larval length. In this thesis, significant negative, additive 
effects between high temperature and oil exposure were detected on larval length, instead of 
antagonistic interactions, indicating that the interaction between these stressors may differ 
across biological endpoints considered. 
Finally, a significant three-way interaction was detected in normal development percentage and 
in larval length, showing both synergistic and additive effects depending on the levels of the 
stressors. However, since these interactions are not widely reported in endpoints rather than 
general survival, further studies would be needed to understand the mechanisms and ecological 
relevance. 
4.3. Greenlandic early development: survival  
All larvae from the Greenlandic population experienced development arrest with no progression 
beyond the trochophore stage. Larval density subsequently decreased until the population 
disappeared. Reproduction in the Arctic is particularly challenging due to low temperatures and 
the strong seasonality. According to Thorson (1950), benthic marine invertebrates at high 
latitudes tend to produce fewer, larger, lecithotrophic larvae rather than numerous 
==PAGE:30==
29 
 
planktotrophic larvae common at lower latitudes, which helps larvae survive severe conditions by 
relying less on food availability. However, later research shows that some polar species still 
produce planktotrophic larvae. Adapted to these conditions, different strategies are employed, 
for instance, hatching independently of the phytoplankton bloom, reducing metabolic costs and 
using alternative food sources, such as dissolved organic matter or bacteria (Clarke, 1992). Studies 
have found that the abundance of larvae from four bivalve species is strongly correlated to food 
availability (related to the spring phytoplankton bloom) in the Arctic (Brandner et al., 2017). This 
suggests that, in the case of Mytilus, the ability to develop under severe conditions may depend 
on other physiological or developmental adaptations rather than differential feeding strategy. 
However, under the laboratory conditions of this thesis larval growth was limited, indicating that 
the environmental requirements were not fulfilled.  Further research is needed to identify the 
optimal conditions required for successful hatching and development of Arctic Mytilus spp. under 
laboratory conditions. 
In this experiment, low salinity and oil exposure alone significantly enhanced trochophore 
survival. When combined or in the presence of high temperature they exerted antagonistic 
interactions, meaning that survival increased, but less than expected with additive effects.  
These results contrast with previous studies that report mainly negative effects of oil exposure 
on survival. For example, Delorenzo et al. (2021) found that mud snails (Tritia obsoleta) were 
more sensitive to oil than higher trophic levels such as shrimp and sheepshead minnows, which 
increased when combined with low salinity, using the same Louisiana sweet crude oil as in this 
thesis. Similarly, Strømgren & Nielsen (1991) reported negative effects of diesel oil on survival of 
M. edulis larvae. Camus et al. (2003) documented negative impacts of oil exposure in adult 
bivalves (Mya truncata), but also a high antioxidant capacity, which is common in polar species 
compared to temperate species. This elevated antioxidant capacity is possibly an evolutive 
adaptation to a highly prooxidant marine environment caused by increased UV radiation 
associated with ozone depletion, combined with a low metabolic rate that implies slow cellular 
turnover (Camus et al., 2003). While this may explain a higher tolerance to oxidative stress, it 
does not necessarily account for a positive effect on survival. 
One possible explanation for the increased survival observed here is hormesis. Hormetic effects 
have been reported in molluscs, where oil exposures have been shown to stimulate growth or 
settlement (Almeda et al., 2023; Strømgren et al., 1986) 
Regarding salinity, the apparent positive effect of 24 PSU on survival might be explained by this 
level being closer to the optimal salinity for this particular Mytilus spp. population during early 
==PAGE:31==
30 
 
development. A plausible explanation is acclimation to locally reduced salinities, likely driven by 
ice melt and continental freshwater inputs during the developmental period. Although previous 
work on other Mytilus species generally reported better responses at higher salinities (e.g., 
Bayne, 1965; Hrs-Brenko et al., 1977; Nadella et al., 2013), these discrepancies likely reflect 
population-specific adaptations to local salinity regimes. Salinity tolerance can vary widely among 
populations, influencing their responses to additional stressors such as oil exposure. 
In this experiment, the interactions between temperature and oil, and between salinity and oil, 
were antagonistic, meaning the combined negative effect was less than expected but still 
harmful. This could mean that these stressors trigger similar defense responses, so when 
combined, the effect is less than expected. The interaction between temperature and salinity was 
additive, meaning their effects added up because they likely affect larvae through different ways. 
The three-way interaction between temperature, salinity, and oil showed synergistic or additive 
negative effects on survival, suggesting that when all three stressors are combined, larvae face a 
more complex and severe effects on survival. These patterns highlight the importance of 
considering multiple stressor interactions. 
4.4. Veliger experiment 
The results for the veliger experiments did not reveal clear or consistent patterns in larval 
responses to the tested stressors. One possible explanation is the high within-group variability 
typically observed in early developmental stages. Traits such as clearance rate, larval size and 
survival often may vary substantially, potentially masking abrupt treatment effects. This has been 
previously reported in M. edulis, where growth differences from the same batch can be 
pronounced (Bayne, 1965).  
Additionally, the limited number of replicates (n=3), combined with a reduced number of 
individuals per replicate due to high mortality in larval cultures, further constrained the 
robustness of the results. This high mortality may be linked to suboptimal culturing conditions. 
While bivalve larvae are typically reared in large volumes and flow-through systems in hatcheries, 
this thesis used small volumes and closed systems, which may have led to rapid deterioration of 
water quality and compromised larval survival.  
These factors together may have limited or overestimated statistically significant trends and 
complicated the interpretation of multifactorial stressor interactions.  
==PAGE:32==
31 
 
4.4.1. Clearance rate 
The mean control clearance rate observed in this experiment (0.165 mL ind-1 day-1) aligns with 
values reported for veliger larvae in the literature. Bayne (1965) reported clearance rates ranging 
from 0.11 to 0.62 mL ind-1 day-1 for M. edulis larvae (210-260 µm) fed with Isochrysis galbana. 
Clearance rate of M. edulis larvae was not substantially affected by salinity, temperature or oil 
exposure when each stressor was applied individually. In contrast, Bayne (1965) reported that an 
increase of temperature (11 – 18°C) led to an increase of clearance rate, while Gardner & 
Thompson (2001) observed a reduction of clearance rate (in adults) at lower salinities (11-29 
PSU). The lack of effect observed in larvae may be related to a broader physiological tolerance at  
this developmental stage, as temperature and salinity tend to have effects only outside the 
optimal range (Brenko & Calabrese, 1969; Hrs-Brenko et al., 1977).  
Interestingly, an antagonistic, positive effect of high temperature and oil was observed on the 
clearance rate. Whereas a moderate increase of the temperature could accelerate metabolic 
processes and result in higher clearance rates (Gardner & Thompson, 2001), oil is generally 
associated with negative effects on fertilization, normal development, growth, and survival 
(Baussant et al., 2011; Saco-Álvarez et al., 2008; Strømgren & Nielsen, 1991) on M. edulis and 
other species. This positive interaction might reflect an hormetic response, as previously 
observed in M. edulis with growth stimulation (Strømgren et al., 1986) and in other marine 
invertebrate species with settlement stimulation (Almeda et al., 2023). 
Other studies have shown that anthropogenic pollutants, such as cadmium, can negatively affect 
ingestion rate (Diaz et al., 2018). In this experiment, the combination of low salinity and oil 
exposure interacted synergistically, reducing the clearance rate more than expected with additive 
effects. Similar interactions have been reported for early life stages of meroplanktonic molluscs 
exposed to different salinities in combination with oil (Delorenzo et al., 2021), UV filters (Cuccaro 
et al., 2022) and microplastics (Boukadida et al., 2024) suggesting that suboptimal salinity may 
increase sensitivity to a range of contaminants during early development. 
The combination of all three stressors (temperature, salinity and oil) did not show substantial 
effects nor interactions. The lack of observed effects in this case could be related to the high 
variability and experimental limitations, potentially masking complex responses and interactions.  
4.4.2. Veliger length 
Neither high temperature, low salinity nor oil exposure had a substantial effect on the length of 
veliger larvae at 4 weeks of age, after 1 week of exposure. In contrast, Bayne (1965) observed a 
general increase in larval growth rate between 10 and 22°C. However, Lazo & Pita (2012) reported 
==PAGE:33==
32 
 
no significant differences in D-veliger growth in M. galloprovicialis at 18, 20 and 24°C, which is 
consistent to the results of this thesis.  
Several studies demonstrated reduced shell growth under oil exposure (Baussant et al., 2011; 
Strømgren et al., 1986; Strømgren & Nielsen, 1991). However, Strømgren et al. (1986) found no 
negative effects of growth under 0.12 mg L-1 of encapsulated oil exposure, which could explain 
the lack of effect observed in this thesis. 
Moreover, substantial intra-cohort variability in larval size may influence the results. Bayne (1965) 
noted considerable growth variation even within larvae originating from a single pair of parents 
(from 149 ± 6 to 226 ± 18 µm). 
In contrast, the combination of high temperature and oil exposure showed a synergistic 
interaction, significantly reducing larval length. This synergistic interaction on M. galloprovincialis 
larval growth was also observed when high temperature is combined with lead (Hrs-Brenko et al., 
1977). Similar interactions between high temperature and other pollutants, such as metals 
(Boukadida et al., 2016, 2021; Mlouka et al., 2025) or nanoparticles (Dose et al., 2024), have also 
been reported in different early developmental stages of Mytilus spp, such as fertilization, normal 
development and swimming behaviour.  
4.4.3. Survival 
Veliger survival was primarily negatively impacted by high oil exposure alone. Negative effects of 
oil leading to a reduced survival has been reported by other studies in M. edulis (Strømgren & 
Nielsen, 1991) but also in different organisms (Delorenzo et al., 2021). 
While neither high temperature nor low salinity individually affected survival, their two-way 
combinations exhibited varied influences, ranging from additive negative effects to synergistic 
and antagonistic interactions. The three-way interactions were complex and lacked a consistent 
pattern across factor levels, showing both synergistic and antagonistic effects depending on the 
specific combination of temperature, salinity, and oil concentrations. This lack of predictability is 
also observed in other studies investigating combined effects of multiple stressors (e.g. 
Boukadida et al., 2021; Delorenzo et al., 2021; Dose et al., 2024), highlighting the importance of 
testing multi-stressor scenarios to better understand ecotoxicological risks under realistic 
environmental conditions.  
In this thesis, the inconsistent patterns were most likely influenced by the experimental 
limitations mentioned earlier, including high within-group variability, low replication, and the 
reduced number of individuals per replicate. 
==PAGE:34==
33 
 
4.5. Pediveliger settlement experiment 
Larval settlement is a critical stage for marine invertebrates. As described by (Bayne, 1965), this 
process includes the foot protrusion, velum retraction, crawling and the eventual secretion of the 
byssus thread for attachment. Several studies have investigated the microbial composition of 
biofilms and chemical cues influencing larval settlement (Bao et al., 2007; Rato et al., 2025). 
However, the role of the physical substrate itself is often overlooked. In this thesis, larvae showed 
a low preference (52%) for settling directly on the experimental biofilm. This may be explained by 
a greater preference to settle on filamentous substrates, as previously reported in laboratory 
settings, specially, a preference for silk over nylon filaments, possibly due to fine hairs stimulating 
algal growth (Bayne, 1965).  
The results demonstrate that high temperature and oil exposure were the primary factors 
negatively affecting larval settlement. Environmental factors can significantly modulate the 
process of metamorphosis. While M. edulis are able to delay metamorphosis up to 45 days at 
16°C, increasing temperature (between 21 and 22°C) has been shown to decrease in this delay 
(Bayne, 1965). This delay is considered a potentially adaptative strategy to ensure settlement in 
suitable environments for survival, growth and reproduction (Almeda et al., 2023). However, it is 
associated with a cost. As larvae transition to the pediveliger stage, they begin reabsorbing the 
velum which significantly reduces even completely inhibits their feeding capacity (Bayne, 1965). 
Any factor that delays metamorphosis will impact negatively in their survival (Widdows, 1991). 
Since metamorphosis requires a high energy expenditure at a time when individuals have limited 
feeding ability, success is likely linked to having sufficient energy reserves, particularly from high-
quality lipid-rich food sources (Toupoint et al., 2012). 
Almeda et al., (2023) observed metamorphosis ratio at control conditions after 6 days remained 
lower than 20% without oil exposure, which are similar to the values obtained in the control 
treatment of this thesis. 
In this experiment, settlement was substantially reduced at 21.5°C. This is supported by Bayne 
(1965) who observed specific temperature limits for metamorphosis to be 19.8°C and 20.8°C for 
M. edulis from different sites.  
The presence of oil negatively affected the settlement process. In contrast to our findings,  
Almeda et al. (2023) observed induced metamorphosis in two benthic species (Dendraster 
excentricus and Rissoa sp.), although an explanation for that is enhanced proliferation of bacteria 
that produce metamorphic cues, which may be limited under these experimental conditions. 
==PAGE:35==
34 
 
However, pollutants such as oil are generally known to inhibit metamorphosis in marine 
invertebrates, including oysters and abalones (Zhang, 2002). 
In conclusion, larval settlement was mainly impaired by high temperature and oil exposure, with 
additive negative effects when combined with low salinity. These results highlight the sensitivity 
of this stage to multiple stressors acting together. 
4.6. Ecological implications 
The combined effects of high temperature, low salinity, and oil contamination on Mytilus have 
important ecological implications. Stressors during early life stages can reduce hatching, larval 
development, growth, survival and settlement, leading to reduced recruitment and smaller adult 
populations. A reduction in Mytilus population size may impair their key ecological functions, such 
as water filtration and nutrient recycling, potentially altering mineralization processes. This may 
affect nutrient availability and primary productivity in coastal ecosystems (Nielsen & Maar, 2007). 
Moreover, reduced Mytilus abundance would reduce food supply for higher trophic-level species 
(Brandner et al., 2017), potentially disrupting the entire food web. Understanding the effects of 
these combined stressors on larval stages is important to predict how future environmental 
changes will impact Mytilus populations and their broader marine ecosystem.  
The experimental conditions used in this thesis reflect environmentally relevant scenarios for the 
populations investigated. Temperature and salinity ranges were based on long-term field data 
from the original ecosystem of the populations accounting for natural variability during the 
spawning and larval development period. The selected temperatures also match projected 
increase under moderate to pessimistic future climate change scenarios (Intergovernmental 
Panel on Climate Change, 2023). Moreover, oil concentrations were based on realistic scenarios 
and remained below the thresholds of international discharge regulations, representing 
contamination levels typical of coastal environments affected by shipping and oil extraction 
activities (International Maritime Organization, 2024). Therefore, the combined stressor 
treatments provide a realistic framework to assess the potential impacts of climate change and 
pollution on Mytilus early life stages in their natural habitats. 
This thesis shows that the most sensitive larval stage is the D-shaped larvae, with normal 
development reduced to approximately 20% at 19ºC and nearly absent at 21.5ºC under 33 PSU 
salinities, with the presence of oil further intensifying these effects. Although climate change is 
expected to advance the spring phytoplankton bloom (Doney et al., 2012), potentially leading to 
earlier spawning events under cooler sea temperatures, this could benefit reproductive success 
and recruitment, as later larval stages appear to be more thermotolerant.  
==PAGE:36==
35 
 
However, if the average sea surface temperatures increase during the early larval development 
period (April and May), as projected under climate change scenarios (Intergovernmental Panel 
on Climate Change, 2023), Mytilus larvae may face critical development difficulties, leading to 
lower recruitment success. This may help explain the limitations of a second spawning peak in 
summer resulting in difficulties in successful recruitment due to elevated sea temperatures 
(Riisgård et al., 2015).  
Under such context, Mytilus may either adapt to higher temperatures, facing physiological trade-
offs to maintain heat tolerance (Clarke, 2003), or be progressively replaced by more 
thermotolerant species such as M. galloprovincialis (Boukadida et al., 2021). While this 
replacement may not lead to a complete loss of ecosystem function due to the ecological 
redundancy between species, it would still modify biogeographic patterns and diversity 
potentially affecting ecosystem composition and resilience.  
5. Conclusion 
Overall, the results support the hypotheses proposed at the start of the thesis, demonstrating 
that high temperature and oil exposure are the major drivers negatively affecting multiple early 
life stages of M. edulis, including fertilization, larval development, survival, and settlement. In 
contrast, low salinity did not consistently cause negative effects and in some cases mitigated 
stressor impacts, especially during D-shaped larval development. 
The Greenlandic population showed greater resilience during fertilization compared to the Danish 
population, being less affected by temperature and oil exposure, even showing enhanced 
fertilization and survival under oil exposure. These contrasting responses between populations 
suggest that local adaptation or phenotypic plasticity influences tolerance to environmental 
stressors. However, larval development was not completed under laboratory conditions for the 
Greenlandic population, highlighting the sensitivity of this stage. Likewise, the first larval stage 
after fertilization (D-shaped larvae) in the Danish population appeared to be the most sensitive, 
where the combined effects of high temperature and oil exposure completely impaired normal 
development, a pattern further supported by abnormality types and reduced larval length at this 
stage. 
Later larval stages, such as veliger and pediveliger larvae, followed the general pattern of negative 
effects from high temperature and oil, but also showed greater variability in responses, which 
may partly reflect experimental limitations such as low replication and small numbers of 
individuals. Despite these limitations, the findings demonstrate the vulnerability of Mytilus larval 
stages to combined environmental stressors, indicating potentially increased population risks 
==PAGE:37==
36 
 
under future climate change and pollution scenarios. This emphasizes the need for continued 
research to understand better mechanisms of tolerance and to improve predictions of 
recruitment success and ecosystem impacts.

heaters connected to Inkbird temperature controllers, which measured the temperature through 
a sensor. In parallel, temperature was monitored with data loggers and an air pump ensured even 
heat distribution. Vials or bottles, containing the corresponding salinity and oil concentration, 
were submerged in the baths. (b) Detail of the setup, showing the temperature sensor, the data 
logger, the heater, the air pump, and the bottles submerged in the water baths. ....................... 58

A. Background and experimental setup 
A1. 
 Systematic review on combined effects of climate change and 
anthropogenic pollutants on early stages of meroplanktonic species 
A1.1.  Research terms 
On 14th of January 2025, a literature search in Web of Science (WoS) using search terms combining 
anthropogenic pollutants, climate change stressors and marine larval invertebrates:  
(contamin* OR pollut* OR oil OR chemical* OR debris OR microplastic* OR metal* OR toxic* OR 
hydrocarbon* OR pesticide* OR pharmaceutical* OR PFAS OR nanoparticle*) AND (“climate 
change” OR “global warming” OR warming OR acidification OR freshening OR temperature* OR 
heatwave* OR hypoxi*) AND (ocean* OR marine OR sea) AND (larva* OR meroplankton* OR 
embryo*) AND (mollusc* OR bivalve* OR gastropod* OR polychaet* OR crustacean* OR 
echinoderm* OR urchin* OR tunicat* OR bryozoa* OR phoronid* OR platyhelminthe* OR 
brachiopod* OR mussel* OR clam* OR crab* OR snail* OR lobster* OR “sea star*” OR starfish* 
OR shrimp* OR barnacle* OR coral* OR sponge*) 
This research initially returned 919 scientific articles. After applying specific inclusion criteria: 1) 
Studies on meroplanktonic organisms, 2) endpoints focusing on larval stages, and 3) experimental 
designs combining an environmental stressor with an anthropogenic pollutant, a total of 78 
relevant papers were selected.

Figure 10. Experimental setup: water baths built in portable insulated containers. (a) Greenlandic 
temperature treatments were placed inside a fridge. Water temperature was regulated using heaters 
connected to Inkbird temperature controllers, which measured the temperature through a sensor. In 
parallel, temperature was monitored with data loggers and an air pump ensured even heat distribution. 
Vials or bottles, containing the corresponding salinity and oil concentration, were submerged in the 
baths. (b) Detail of the setup, showing the temperature sensor, the data logger, the heater, the air 
pump, and the bottles submerged in the water baths.

B2.3.3.  Length 
Table 16. Raw data for length (µm) of Danish M. edulis under different temperatures (17, 19 and 21.5°C),  salinities (24 
and 33 PSU), oil exposures (0, 1 and 10 µL L-1) measured after 72 hours of exposure post-fertilization (n=3). Columns 
include replicate per treatment (Rep.), temperature, salinity, oil concentration and individual larval length.

B2.5.2. Length 
Table 21. Raw data of veliger length (mµ) of 4-week-old Danish M. edulis under different temperatures (17, 19 and 
21.5°C),  salinities (24 and 33 PSU), oil exposures (0, 1 and 10 µL L-1) measured after 1 week of exposure (n=3). Columns 
include replicate number (Rep.), temperature, salinity, oil concentration and larval length.

C. Statistical models and model output 
Throughout this appendix, environmental variables are represented by coded identifiers in the 
model outputs for simplicity. The precise definitions and corresponding physical values of temp_id 
(temperature), sal_id (salinity), and oil_id (oil exposure) are provided in Appendix B.2, Raw data. 
C1. 
Statistical models 
C1.1. Bayesian model 
For Danish and Greenlandic fertilization, normal development, abnormality type, Greenlandic 
and Danish survival, clearance rate and settlement. 
The same model structure was applied across multiple experiments, with the response variable 
adapted to each case. Specifically, a binomial model with a logit link function was used for 
proportional data, such as fertilization rate (Danish and Greenlandic; %), normal development (%), 
abnormality type (%), survival (Danish and Greenlandic; %) and settlement (%).  
As an example, the model used for fertilization rate was: 
model_brm <- brm(fert_eggs | trials(tot_eggs) ~ temp_id * sal_id * oil_id, 
                 data = fert_results_21_03, 
                 family = binomial(link = "logit"), 
==PAGE:115==
114 
 
                 cores = 4,    
                 chains = 4,  
                 iter = 4000) 
Environmental variables were coded as temp_id, sal_id, and oil_id (Appendix B.2), and models 
included all possible interactions. The models assessing abnormality types were run with 8000 
iterations to ensure better convergence. For clearance rate (mL ind⁻¹ day⁻¹), which followed a 
normal distribution (Lillifors test p=0.5), a default Gaussian distribution was applied.  
C1.2. GAMLSS model  
For D-shaped larvae and veliger larvae length  
For D-shape and veliger larval length, a GAMLSS model was fitted using a Skew t type 1 distribution 
(ST1), with the mean (mu) modelled as a function of temp_id, sal_id, and oil_id including all 
interactions. Dispersion (sigma) and shape parameters (nu) were held constant: 
model_gamlss <- gamlss(Length ~ temp_id * sal_id * oil_id, sigma.formula = ~ 1, nu.formula = ~ 
1, family = ST1, data = length_veliger, trace = TRUE) 
C1.3. Post – hoc analysis 
To assess pairwise comparisons between levels of temperature, salinity, and oil exposure, a set of 
model contrasts was defined using the emmeans package in R. Seven different designs were 
tested, including full interaction (temp * sal * oil) and combinations isolating the effect of each 
variable while controlling for the others (e.g., temp_id | sal_id * oil_id). For each design, estimated 
marginal means were computed, and pairwise contrasts were extracted and summarized as odds 
ratios with 95% highest posterior density (HPD) intervals for Bayesian models, and estimates with 
p-values for GAMLSS models. Example: 
designs <- list( 
  temp_sal_oil = ~ temp_id * sal_id * oil_id, 
  temp       = ~ temp_id | sal_id * oil_id, 
  sal        = ~ sal_id  | temp_id * oil_id, 
  oil        = ~ oil_id  | temp_id * sal_id, 
  temp_sal   = ~ temp_id * sal_id | oil_id, 
  temp_oil   = ~ temp_id * oil_id | sal_id, 
==PAGE:116==
115 
 
  sal_oil    = ~ sal_id  * oil_id | temp_id) 
coef<- c("contrast", "odds.ratio", "lower.HPD", "upper.HPD", "temp_id", "sal_id", "oil_id") 
results <- list() 
 
for (n in names(designs)) { 
  em <- emmeans(model_brm, specs = designs[[n]], type = "response") 
  con <- pairs(em, reverse =TRUE) 
  res <- summary(con) 
  cols <- coef[coef %in% names(res)] 
  results[[n]] <- dplyr::select(res, all_of(cols))} 
final <-bind_rows(results, .id = "Parameter") 
C2. 
Model summary and pairwise coefficients 
Only odds ratios are shown for all pairwise comparisons. Probabilities were calculated for selected 
cases and are discussed in the main text where relevant.
